<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Metric system</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a topical guide to this subject, see <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_the_metric_system" title="Outline of the metric system">Outline of the metric system</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a more accessible and less technical introduction to this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Introduction_to_the_metric_system" title="Introduction to the metric system">Introduction to the metric system</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FourMetricInstruments.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" height="347" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/FourMetricInstruments.JPG/260px-FourMetricInstruments.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/FourMetricInstruments.JPG/390px-FourMetricInstruments.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/FourMetricInstruments.JPG/520px-FourMetricInstruments.JPG 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FourMetricInstruments.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Four metric measuring devices: a <a href="/wiki/Tape_measure" title="Tape measure">tape measure</a> in <a href="/wiki/Centimetre" title="Centimetre">centimetres</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Thermometer" title="Thermometer">thermometer</a> in <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">degrees Celsius</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilogram</a> weight and a <a href="/wiki/Multimeter" title="Multimeter">multimeter</a> that measures <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">volts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">amperes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ohm" title="Ohm">ohms</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>metric system</b> is an internationally agreed <a href="/wiki/Decimal" title="Decimal">decimal</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systems_of_measurement" title="Systems of measurement">system of measurement</a>. It was originally based on the <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M%C3%A8tre_des_Archives" title="Mètre des Archives">mètre des Archives</a></i></span> and the <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilogramme_des_Archives" title="Kilogramme des Archives">kilogramme des Archives</a></i></span> introduced by the <a href="/wiki/French_First_Republic" title="French First Republic">French First Republic</a> in 1799,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-1">[1]</a></sup> but over the years the definitions of the metre and the kilogram have been refined, and the metric system has been extended to incorporate many more units. Although a number of variants of the metric system emerged in the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries, the term is now often used as a synonym for "SI"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-French_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-French-2">[Note 1]</a></sup> or the "<a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">International System of Units</a>"—the official <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systems_of_measurement" title="Systems of measurement">system of measurement</a> in almost every country in the world.</p>
<p>The metric system has been officially sanctioned for use in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> since 1866,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H.R._596_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-H.R._596-3">[2]</a></sup> but the U.S. remains the only <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">industrialised country</a> that has not fully adopted the metric system as its official system of measurement, although in 1988 the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> passed the <a href="/wiki/Omnibus_Foreign_Trade_and_Competitiveness_Act" title="Omnibus Foreign Trade and Competitiveness Act">Omnibus Foreign Trade and Competitiveness Act</a>, which designates "the metric system of measurement as the preferred system of weights and measures for U.S. trade and commerce". Among many other things, the act requires federal agencies to use metric measurements in nearly all of its activities, although there are still some exceptions allowing traditional linear units to be used in documents intended for consumers. Many sources also cite the countries of <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a>) as the only other countries not to have done so. Although the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> also uses the metric system for most administrative and trade purposes, <a href="/wiki/Imperial_units" title="Imperial units">imperial units</a> are widely used by the public and are permitted or obligatory for some purposes, such as road signs.</p>
<p>Although the originators intended to devise a system that was equally accessible to all, it proved necessary to use <a href="/wiki/Prototype" title="Prototype">prototype</a> units in the custody of national or local authorities as standards. Control of the prototype units of measure was maintained by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_French_Republic" title="Second French Republic">later French governments</a> until 1875, when it was passed to an international <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_organisation" title="Intergovernmental organisation">intergovernmental organisation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">General Conference on Weights and Measures</a> (CGPM).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-French_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-French-2">[Note 1]</a></sup></p>
<p>From its beginning, the main features of the metric system were the standard set of interrelated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Base_units" title="Base units">base units</a> and a standard set of prefixes in powers of ten. These base units are used to derive larger and smaller units that could replace a huge number of other units of measure in existence. Although the system was first developed for commercial use, the development of <a href="/wiki/Coherence_(units_of_measurement)" title="Coherence (units of measurement)">coherent</a> units of measure made it particularly suitable for science and engineering.</p>
<p>The uncoordinated use of the metric system by different scientific and engineering disciplines, particularly in the late 19th century, resulted in different choices of <a href="/wiki/Base_unit_(measurement)" title="Base unit (measurement)">base units</a> even though all were based on the same definitions of the units of the <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">metre</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilogram</a>. During the 20th century, efforts were made to rationalise these units, and in 1960, the CGPM published the <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">International System of Units</a>, which has since then been the <a href="/wiki/International_standard" title="International standard">internationally recognised standard</a> metric system.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Features">Features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although the metric system has changed and developed since its inception, its basic concepts have hardly changed. Designed for transnational use, it consisted of a basic set of units of measurement, now known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Base_units" title="Base units">base units</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Derived_units" title="Derived units">Derived units</a> were built up from the base units using logical rather than empirical relationships while multiples and submultiples of both base and derived units were decimal-based and identified by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI_prefixes" title="SI prefixes">standard set of prefixes</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Universality">Universality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Universality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PRC_Expressway_RoadSign_Distances.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="479" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/PRC_Expressway_RoadSign_Distances.jpg/220px-PRC_Expressway_RoadSign_Distances.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/PRC_Expressway_RoadSign_Distances.jpg/330px-PRC_Expressway_RoadSign_Distances.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/PRC_Expressway_RoadSign_Distances.jpg/440px-PRC_Expressway_RoadSign_Distances.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PRC_Expressway_RoadSign_Distances.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chinese road sign listing distances on an <a href="/wiki/Expressways_of_Beijing" title="Expressways of Beijing">expressway in eastern Beijing</a>. Although the primary text is in Chinese, the distances use internationally recognised characters.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> in 1789, most countries and even some cities had their own <a href="/wiki/System_of_measurement" title="System of measurement">system of measurement</a>. Although different countries might have used units of measure with the same name, such as the <a href="/wiki/Foot_(unit)" title="Foot (unit)">foot</a>, or local language equivalents such as <i>pied</i>, <i>Fuß,</i> and <i>voet</i>, there was no consistency in the magnitude of those units, nor in the relationships with their multiples and submultiples,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palaiseau_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Palaiseau-4">[3]</a></sup> much like the modern-day differences between the US and the UK <a href="/wiki/Pint" title="Pint">pints</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gallon" title="Gallon">gallons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The metric system was designed to be universal—in the words of the French philosopher <a href="/wiki/Marquis_de_Condorcet" title="Marquis de Condorcet">Marquis de Condorcet</a> it was to be "for all people for all time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:1</sup> It was designed for ordinary people, for engineers who worked in human-related measurements and for astronomers and physicists who worked with numbers both small and large, hence the huge range of the prefixes that have been defined in SI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SI_prefix_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-SI_prefix-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the French Government first investigated the idea of overhauling their system of measurement, the concept of universality was put into practice in 1789: <a href="/wiki/Charles_Maurice_de_Talleyrand-P%C3%A9rigord" title="Charles Maurice de Talleyrand-Périgord">Maurice de Talleyrand</a>, acting on Condorcet's advice, invited <a href="/wiki/John_Riggs_Miller" title="John Riggs Miller">John Riggs Miller</a>, a British parliamentarian and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, the American Secretary of State to <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>, to work with the French in producing an international standard by promoting legislation in their respective legislative bodies. However, these early overtures failed and the custody of the metric system remained in the hands of the French government until 1875.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:250–253</sup></p>
<p>In languages where the distinction is made, unit names are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_noun" title="Common noun">common nouns</a> (i.e. not proper nouns). They use the character set and follow the grammatical rules of the language concerned, for example <i>"<a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/kilom%C3%A8tre" title="fr:kilomètre">kilomètre</a>"</i>, "<i><a class="extiw" href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/kil%C3%B3metro" title="es:kilómetro">kilómetro</a></i>", but each unit has a symbol that is independent of language, for example "km" for "kilometre", "V" for "volts" etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noun_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-noun-7">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decimal_multiples">Decimal multiples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Decimal multiples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Metric_prefix" title="Metric prefix">metric prefix</a></div>
<p>In the metric system, multiples and submultiples of units follow a decimal pattern,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[Note 2]</a></sup> a concept identified as a possibility in 1586 by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Stevin" title="Simon Stevin">Simon Stevin</a>, the Flemish mathematician who had introduced decimal fractions into Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stevin_MacTutor_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stevin_MacTutor-9">[7]</a></sup> This is done at the cost of losing the simplicity associated with many traditional systems of units where division by 3 does not result in awkward fractions; for example one third of a foot is four inches, a simplicity that in 1790 was debated, but rejected by the originators of the metric system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glasser_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glasser-10">[8]</a></sup> In 1854, in the introduction to the proceedings of the [British] Decimal Association, the mathematician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustus_de_Morgan" title="Augustus de Morgan">Augustus de Morgan</a> summarised the advantages of a decimal-based system over a non-decimal system thus: "In the <i>simple</i> rules of <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic" title="Arithmetic">arithmetic</a>, we practice a pure decimal system, nowhere interrupted by the entrance of any other system: <i>from column to column we never carry anything but tens</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[9]</a></sup></p>
<table cellpadding="1" class="infobox" style="padding:0; text-align:left;">

<tr>
<td colspan="4" style="background:#ddd; text-align:center">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Common_metric_prefixes" title="Template:Common metric prefixes"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Common_metric_prefixes" title="Template talk:Common metric prefixes"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Common_metric_prefixes&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>

</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>A common set of decimal-based prefixes that have the effect of multiplication or division by an integer power of ten can be applied to units that are themselves too large or too small for practical use. The concept of using consistent classical (<a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a>) names for the prefixes was first proposed in a report by the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">[French Revolutionary]</a> Commission on Weights and Measures in May 1793.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:89–96</sup> The prefix <i>kilo</i>, for example, is used to multiply the unit by 1000, and the prefix <i>milli</i> is to indicate a one-thousandth part of the unit. Thus the <i><a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilogram</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">kilometre</a></i> are a thousand <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">grams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">metres</a> respectively, and a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milligram" title="Milligram">milligram</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Millimetre" title="Millimetre">millimetre</a></i> are one thousandth of a gram and metre respectively. These relations can be written symbolically as:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SI_prefix_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-SI_prefix-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>1 mg = 0.001 g</dd>
<dd>1 km = 1000 m</dd>
</dl>
<p>In the early days, multipliers that were positive powers of ten were given Greek-derived prefixes such as <i>kilo-</i> and <i>mega-</i>, and those that were negative powers of ten were given Latin-derived prefixes such as <i>centi-</i> and <i>milli-</i>. However, 1935 extensions to the prefix system did not follow this convention: the prefixes <i>nano-</i> and <i>micro-</i>, for example have Greek roots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[10]</a></sup> During the 19th century the prefix <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-SI_unit_prefix#Obsolete_prefixes" title="Non-SI unit prefix"><i>myria-</i></a>, derived from the Greek word μύριοι (<i>mýrioi</i>), was used as a multiplier for <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7004100000000000000♠</span>10<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>When applying prefixes to derived units of area and volume that are expressed in terms of units of length squared or cubed, the square and cube operators are applied to the unit of length including the prefix, as illustrated below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SI_prefix_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-SI_prefix-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>
<table>
<tr>
<td>1 mm<sup>2</sup> (square millimetre)</td>
<td>= (1 mm)<sup>2</sup> </td>
<td>= (0.001 m)<sup>2</sup> </td>
<td>= <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6994100000000000000♠</span>0.000<span style="margin-left:.25em;">001</span> m<sup>2</sup></span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>1 km<sup>2</sup> (<a href="/wiki/Square_kilometre" title="Square kilometre">square kilometre</a>) </td>
<td>= (1 km)<sup>2</sup></td>
<td>= (1000 m)<sup>2</sup></td>
<td>= <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7006100000000000000♠</span>1<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span> m<sup>2</sup></span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>1 mm<sup>3</sup> (cubic millimetre)</td>
<td>= (1 mm)<sup>3</sup></td>
<td>= (0.001 m)<sup>3</sup></td>
<td>= <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6991100000000000000♠</span>0.000<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">001</span> m<sup>3</sup></span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>1 km<sup>3</sup> (cubic kilometre)</td>
<td>= (1 km)<sup>3</sup></td>
<td>= (1000 m)<sup>3</sup></td>
<td>= <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7009100000000000000♠</span>1<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span> m<sup>3</sup></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>Prefixes are not usually used to indicate multiples of a second greater than 1; the non-SI units of <a href="/wiki/Minute" title="Minute">minute</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hour" title="Hour">hour</a> and <a href="/wiki/Day" title="Day">day</a> are used instead. On the other hand, prefixes are used for multiples of the non-SI unit of volume, the <a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">litre</a> (l, L) such as millilitres (ml).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SI_prefix_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-SI_prefix-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Realisability_and_replicable_prototypes">Realisability and replicable prototypes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Realisability and replicable prototypes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kilometre_definition.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="599" data-file-width="596" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Kilometre_definition.svg/220px-Kilometre_definition.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Kilometre_definition.svg/330px-Kilometre_definition.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Kilometre_definition.svg/440px-Kilometre_definition.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kilometre_definition.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <b>metre</b> was originally defined to be one ten millionth of the distance between the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">Equator</a> through Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-1">[1]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Realisation_(metrology)" title="Realisation (metrology)">Realisation (metrology)</a></div>
<p>The base units used in the metric system must be <a href="/wiki/Realisation_(metrology)" title="Realisation (metrology)">realisable</a>, ideally with reference to natural <a href="/wiki/Phenomenon" title="Phenomenon">phenomena</a> rather than unique <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artifact_(archeology)" title="Artifact (archeology)">artefacts</a>. Each of the base units in SI is accompanied by a <i>mise en pratique</i> [practical realisation] published by the BIPM that describes in detail at least one way in which the base unit can be measured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup> Where possible, definitions of the base units were developed so that any laboratory equipped with proper instruments would be able to realise a standard without reliance on an artefact held by another country. In practice, such realisation is done under the auspices of a <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_of_Legal_Metrology#Conformance_Certification" title="International Organization of Legal Metrology">mutual acceptance arrangement</a> (MAA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Metre_and_kilogram">Metre and kilogram</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Metre and kilogram">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the original version of the metric system the base units could be derived from a specified length (the metre) and the weight [mass] of a specified volume (<span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003100000000000000♠</span>1000</span></sub></span> of a cubic metre) of pure water. Initially the <i>de facto</i> French Government of the day, the <a href="/wiki/National_Constituent_Assembly_(France)" title="National Constituent Assembly (France)"><i>Assemblée nationale constituante</i></a>, considered defining the metre as the length of a <a href="/wiki/Pendulum" title="Pendulum">pendulum</a> that has a period of one second at 45°N and an <a href="/wiki/Altitude" title="Altitude">altitude</a> equal to <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a>. The altitude and latitude were specified to accommodate variations in <a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth" title="Gravity of Earth">gravity</a>; the specified latitude was a compromise between the latitude of <span class="nowrap">London (51° 30'N)</span>, <span class="nowrap">Paris (48° 50'N)</span> and the median parallel of the United States (38°N) to accommodate variations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:94</sup> However the mathematician <a href="/wiki/Jean-Charles_de_Borda" title="Jean-Charles de Borda">Borda</a> persuaded the assembly that a survey having its ends at sea level and based on a meridian that spanned at least 10% of the earth's quadrant would be more appropriate for such a basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:96</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CubeLitre.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="308" data-file-width="385" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/CubeLitre.svg/220px-CubeLitre.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/CubeLitre.svg/330px-CubeLitre.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/CubeLitre.svg/440px-CubeLitre.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CubeLitre.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<b>One litre</b> is equivalent to the volume of a <a href="/wiki/Cube" title="Cube">cube</a> with edges of 10 cm and the <b>kilogram</b> was originally designed to be one litre of water at the <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-16">[14]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The available technology of the 1790s made it impracticable to use these definitions as the basis of the kilogram and the metre, so prototypes that represented these quantities insofar as was practicable were manufactured. On 22 June 1799 these prototypes were adopted as the definitive reference pieces, deposited in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archives_nationales_(France)" title="Archives nationales (France)"><i>Archives nationales</i></a> and became known as the <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M%C3%A8tre_des_Archives" title="Mètre des Archives">mètre des Archives</a></i></span> and the <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilogramme_des_Archives" title="Kilogramme des Archives">kilogramme des Archives</a></i></span>. Copies were made and distributed around France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:266–269</sup> These artefacts were replaced in 1889 by the new prototypes manufactured under international supervision. Insofar as was possible, the new prototypes were exact copies of the original prototypes, but used a later technology to ensure better stability. One of each of the kilogram and metre prototypes were chosen by lot to serve as the definitive international reference piece with the remainder being distributed to signatories of the <a href="/wiki/Metre_Convention" title="Metre Convention">Metre Convention</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup> In 1889 there was no generally accepted theory regarding the nature of light but by 1960 the wavelength of specific <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">light spectra</a> could give a more accurate and reproducible value than a prototype metre. In that year the prototype metre was replaced by a formal definition that defines the metre in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> of specified light spectra. By 1983 it was accepted that the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> in vacuum was <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_relativity" title="Theory of relativity">constant</a> and that this constant provided a more <a href="/wiki/Reproducibility" title="Reproducibility">reproducible</a> procedure for measuring length. Therefore, the metre was redefined in terms of the speed of light. These definitions give a much better reproducibility and also allow anyone, anywhere with a suitably equipped laboratory, to make a standard metre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_base_units">Other base units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Other base units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>None of the other base units rely on a prototype – all are based on phenomena that are directly observable and had been in use for many years before formally becoming part of the metric system.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">second</a> first became a <i>de facto</i> base unit within the metric system when, in 1832, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a> used it, the centimetre and the gram to derive the units associated with values of absolute measurements of the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SI_1_8_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-SI_1_8-19">[17]</a></sup> The second, if based on the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth's rotation">Earth's rotation</a>, is not a constant as the Earth's rotation is slowing down—in 2008 the solar day was 0.002 s longer than in 1820.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup> This had been known for many years; consequently in 1952 the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> (IAU) defined the second in terms of the Earth's rotation in the year 1900. Measurements of time were made using <a href="/wiki/Extrapolation" title="Extrapolation">extrapolation</a> from readings based on <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>. With the launch of SI in 1960, the 11th CGPM adopted the IAU definition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> In the years that followed, <a href="/wiki/Atomic_clock" title="Atomic clock">atomic clocks</a> became significantly more reliable and precise; and in 1968 the 13th CGPM redefined the second in terms of a specific frequency from the <a href="/wiki/Emission_spectrum" title="Emission spectrum">emission spectrum</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium 133</a> atom, a component of atomic clocks. This provided the means to measure the time associated with astronomical phenomena rather than using astronomical phenomena as the basis from which time measurements were made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SI_units_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-SI_units-22">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Centimetre%E2%80%93gram%E2%80%93second_system_of_units" title="Centimetre–gram–second system of units">CGS</a> absolute unit of <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric current</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Abampere" title="Abampere">abampere</a>, had been defined in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a> between two parallel current-carrying wires in 1881.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup> In the 1940s, the <a href="/wiki/International_Electrotechnical_Commission" title="International Electrotechnical Commission">International Electrotechnical Commission</a> adopted an <a href="/wiki/MKS_system_of_units" title="MKS system of units">MKS</a> variant of this definition for the <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">ampere</a>, which was adopted in 1948 by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CGPM" title="CGPM">CGPM</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">Temperature</a> has always been based on observable phenomena—in 1744 the <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">degree Centigrade</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[Note 3]</a></sup> was based on the freezing and boiling points of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup> In 1948 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CGPM" title="CGPM">CGPM</a> adopted the Centigrade scale, renamed it the "Celsius" temperature scale name and defined it in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mole(unit)" title="Mole(unit)">mole</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Candela" title="Candela">candela</a> were accepted by the CGPM in 1971 and 1975 respectively, both had been defined by third parties by reference to phenomena rather than artefacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIPM1975_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-BIPM1975-30">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coherence">Coherence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Coherence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coherence_(units_of_measurement)" title="Coherence (units of measurement)">Coherence (units of measurement)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:James_Clerk_Maxwell.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="398" data-file-width="331" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/James_Clerk_Maxwell.jpg/170px-James_Clerk_Maxwell.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/James_Clerk_Maxwell.jpg/255px-James_Clerk_Maxwell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/42/James_Clerk_Maxwell.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:James_Clerk_Maxwell.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> played a major role in developing the concept of a coherent CGS system and in extending the metric system to include electrical units.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Each variant of the metric system has a degree of coherence—the various derived units are directly related to the base units without the need for intermediate conversion factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup> For example, in a coherent system the units of <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a>, <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">power</a> are chosen so that the equations</p>
<dl>
<dd>
<table>
<tr>
<td><i>force</i></td>
<td><i>= mass</i></td>
<td><i>× acceleration</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><i>energy</i> </td>
<td><i>= force</i></td>
<td><i>× distance</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><i>power</i></td>
<td><i>= energy</i> </td>
<td><i>÷ time</i></td>
</tr>
</table>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>hold without the introduction of unit conversion factors. Once a set of coherent units have been defined, other relationships in physics that use those units will automatically be true. Therefore, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein's</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass-energy_equation" title="Mass-energy equation">mass-energy equation</a>, <i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup>, does not require extraneous constants when expressed in coherent units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Centimetre%E2%80%93gram%E2%80%93second_system_of_units" title="Centimetre–gram–second system of units">CGS system</a> had two units of energy, the <a href="/wiki/Erg" title="Erg">erg</a> that was related to <a href="/wiki/Mechanics" title="Mechanics">mechanics</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">calorie</a> that was related to <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a>; so only one of them (the erg) could bear a coherent relationship to the base units. Coherence was a design aim of SI resulting in only one unit of energy being defined – the <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joule</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SI_units_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-SI_units-22">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>In SI, which is a coherent system, the unit of power is the "<a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watt</a>", which is defined as "<em>one</em> joule per second".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SI_units_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-SI_units-22">[20]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_customary_system" title="US customary system">US customary system</a> of measurement, which is non-coherent, the unit of power is the "<a href="/wiki/Horsepower" title="Horsepower">horsepower</a>", which is defined as "550 foot-pounds per second" (the pound in this context being the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pound-force" title="Pound-force">pound-force</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup> Similarly, neither the US gallon nor the imperial gallon is <em>one</em> cubic foot or <em>one</em> cubic yard— the US gallon is 231 cubic inches and the imperial gallon is 277.42 cubic inches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>The concept of coherence was only introduced into the metric system in the third quarter of the 19th century;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maxwell2_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maxwell2-35">[32]</a></sup> in its original form the metric system was non-coherent—in particular the <a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">litre</a> was 0.001 m<sup>3</sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Hectare#Are" title="Hectare">are</a> (from which the <a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">hectare</a> derives) was 100 m<sup>2</sup>. However the units of mass and length were related to each other through the physical properties of water, the gram having been designed as being the mass of one cubic centimetre of water at its freezing point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-France1795_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-France1795-36">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_metric_system" title="History of the metric system">History of the metric system</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Metrication" title="Metrication">Metrication</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bp_John_Wilkins.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="971" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Bp_John_Wilkins.jpg/170px-Bp_John_Wilkins.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Bp_John_Wilkins.jpg/255px-Bp_John_Wilkins.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Bp_John_Wilkins.jpg/340px-Bp_John_Wilkins.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bp_John_Wilkins.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/John_Wilkins" title="John Wilkins">John Wilkins</a> who, in 1668, published an essay proposing a decimal system of measurement</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1585 the Flemish mathematician <a href="/wiki/Simon_Stevin" title="Simon Stevin">Simon Stevin</a> published a small pamphlet called <i>De Theinde</i> ("the tenth"). Decimal fractions had been employed for the extraction of square roots some five centuries before his time, but nobody used decimal numbers in daily life. Stevin declared that using decimals was so important that the universal introduction of decimal weights, measures and coinage was only a matter of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stevin_MacTutor_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stevin_MacTutor-9">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the earliest proposals for a decimal system in which <a href="/wiki/Length" title="Length">length</a>, <a href="/wiki/Area" title="Area">area</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> were linked to each other was made by <a href="/wiki/John_Wilkins" title="John Wilkins">John Wilkins</a>, first secretary of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> of London in his 1668 essay "<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/An_Essay_towards_a_Real_Character_and_a_Philosophical_Language" title="An Essay towards a Real Character and a Philosophical Language">An Essay towards a Real Character and a Philosophical Language</a></i>". His proposal used a pendulum that had a beat of one second as the basis of the unit of length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup> Two years later, in 1670, <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Mouton" title="Gabriel Mouton">Gabriel Mouton</a>, a French abbot and scientist, proposed a decimal system of length based on the circumference of the Earth. His suggestion was that a unit, the milliare, be defined as a minute of arc along a meridian. He then suggested a system of sub-units, dividing successively by factors of ten into the centuria, decuria, virga, virgula, decima, centesima, and millesima. His ideas attracted interest at the time, and were supported by both <a href="/wiki/Jean_Picard" title="Jean Picard">Jean Picard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a> in 1673, and also studied at the Royal Society in London. In the same year, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Leibniz">Gottfried Leibniz</a> independently made proposals similar to those of Mouton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mouton_MacTutor_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mouton_MacTutor-40">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>In pre-revolutionary Europe, each state had its own system of units of measure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palaiseau_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Palaiseau-4">[3]</a></sup> Some countries, such as Spain and Russia, saw the advantages of harmonising their units of measure with those of their trading partners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metricSpain_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-metricSpain-41">[38]</a></sup> However, vested interests who profited from variations in units of measure opposed this. This was particularly prevalent in France where the huge inconsistency in the size of units of measure was one of the causes that, in 1789, led to the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:2</sup> During the early years of the revolution, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic" title="Academic">savants</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[Note 4]</a></sup> including the <a href="/wiki/Marquis_de_Condorcet" title="Marquis de Condorcet">Marquis de Condorcet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Simon_Laplace" title="Pierre-Simon Laplace">Pierre-Simon Laplace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adrien-Marie_Legendre" title="Adrien-Marie Legendre">Adrien-Marie Legendre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Charles_de_Borda" title="Jean-Charles de Borda">Jean-Charles de Borda</a> set up a Commission of Weights and Measures. The commission was of the opinion that the country should adopt a completely new system of measure based on the principles of logic and natural phenomena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[39]</a></sup> Logic dictated that such a system should be based on the <a href="/wiki/Radix" title="Radix">radix</a> used for counting. Their report of March 1791 to the <i>Assemblée nationale constituante</i> considered but rejected the view of Laplace that a <a href="/wiki/Duodecimal" title="Duodecimal">duodecimal</a> system of counting should replace the existing decimal system; the view such a system was bound to fail prevailed. The commission's final recommendation was that the assembly should promote a decimal-based system of measurement. The leaders of the assembly accepted the views of the commission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:99–100</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glasser_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glasser-10">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Initially France attempted to work with other countries towards the adoption of a common set of units of measure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:99–100</sup> Among the supporters of such an international system of units was <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> who, in 1790, presented a document <i><a href="/wiki/Plan_for_Establishing_Uniformity_in_the_Coinage,_Weights,_and_Measures_of_the_United_States" title="Plan for Establishing Uniformity in the Coinage, Weights, and Measures of the United States">Plan for Establishing Uniformity in the Coinage, Weights, and Measures of the United States</a></i> to Congress in which he advocated a decimal system that used traditional names for units (such as ten inches per foot).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[40]</a></sup> The report was considered but not adopted by Congress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_1-11"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">: 249–250</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Poids_et_mesures.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1245" data-file-width="693" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Poids_et_mesures.png/170px-Poids_et_mesures.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Poids_et_mesures.png/255px-Poids_et_mesures.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Poids_et_mesures.png/340px-Poids_et_mesures.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Poids_et_mesures.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Woodcut dated 1800 illustrating the new decimal units that became the legal norm across all France on 4 November 1800</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_metric_system">Original metric system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Original metric system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The French law of 18 Germinal, Year III (7 April 1795) defined five units of measure:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-France1795_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-France1795-36">[33]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">mètre</a></i> for length</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Hectare#Are" title="Hectare"><i>are</i></a> (100 m<sup>2</sup>) for area [of land]</li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Stere" title="Stere">stère</a></i> (1 m<sup>3</sup>) for volume of stacked<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[41]</a></sup> firewood</li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">litre</a></i> (1 dm<sup>3</sup>) for volumes of liquid</li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">gramme</a></i> for mass.</li>
</ul>
<p>This system continued the tradition of having separate base units for geometrically related dimensions, e.g., <i><a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">mètre</a></i> for lengths, <a href="/wiki/Hectare#Are" title="Hectare"><i>are</i></a> (100 m<sup>2</sup>) for areas, <i><a href="/wiki/Stere" title="Stere">stère</a></i> (1 m<sup>3</sup>) for dry capacities, and <i><a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">litre</a></i> (1 dm<sup>3</sup>) for liquid capacities. The <i><a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">hectare</a></i>, equal to a hundred <i><a href="/wiki/Hectare#Are" title="Hectare">ares</a></i>, the area of a square 100 metres on a side (about 2.47 <a href="/wiki/Acre" title="Acre">acres</a>), is still in use. The early metric system included only a few <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI_prefix" title="SI prefix">prefixes</a> from <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milli" title="Milli">milli</a></i> (one thousandth) to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myria" title="Myria">myria</a></i> (ten thousand).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-France1795_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-France1795-36">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Originally the <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram"><i>kilogramme</i></a>, defined as being one <i>pinte</i> (later renamed the <i>litre</i>) of water at the melting point of ice, was called the <i>grave</i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-16">[14]</a></sup> the <i>gramme</i> being an alternative name for a thousandth of a <i>grave</i>. However, the word <i>grave</i>, being a synonym for the title "<a href="/wiki/Count" title="Count">count</a>", had aristocratic connotations and was renamed the <i>kilogramme</i>. The name <i>mètre</i> was suggested by <a href="/wiki/Auguste-Savinien_Leblond" title="Auguste-Savinien Leblond">Auguste-Savinien Leblond</a> in May 1790.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_1-12"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">: 92</sup></p>
<p>France officially adopted the metric system on 10 December 1799. Although it was decreed that its use was to be mandatory in Paris that year and across the provinces the following year, the decree was not universally observed across France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICB_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-NICB-46">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_adoption">International adoption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: International adoption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Metrication" title="Metrication">Metrication</a></div>
<p>Areas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleonic_France" title="Napoleonic France">annexed by France</a> during the Napoleonic era were the first to inherit the metric system. In 1812, Napoleon introduced a system known as <i><a href="/wiki/Mesures_usuelles" title="Mesures usuelles">mesures usuelles</a></i>, which used the names of pre-metric units of measure, but defined them in terms of metric units – for example, the <i>livre metrique</i> (metric pound) was 500 g and the <i>toise metrique</i> (metric fathom) was 2 metres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fevier_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fevier-47">[43]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a> in 1815, France lost the territories that she had annexed; some, such as the <a href="/wiki/Papal_States" title="Papal States">Papal States</a> reverted to their pre-revolutionary units of measure, others such as <a href="/wiki/Baden" title="Baden">Baden</a> adopted a modified version of the <i>mesures usuelles</i>, but France kept her system of measurement intact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-europa1842_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-europa1842-48">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1817, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> reintroduced the metric system, but used <a href="/wiki/Dutch_units_of_measurement" title="Dutch units of measurement">pre-revolutionary names</a>—for example 1 centimetre became the <i>duim</i> (thumb), the <i>ons</i> (ounce) became 100 g and so on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[45]</a></sup> Certain German states adopted similar systems<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-europa1842_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-europa1842-48">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[46]</a></sup> and in 1852 the German <a href="/wiki/Zollverein" title="Zollverein">Zollverein</a> (customs union) adopted the zollpfund (customs pound) of 500 g for intrastate commerce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zollmuseum_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zollmuseum-51">[47]</a></sup> In 1872 the newly formed <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> adopted the metric system as its official system of weights and measures<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[48]</a></sup> and the newly formed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(1861%E2%80%931946)" title="Kingdom of Italy (1861–1946)">Kingdom of Italy</a> likewise, following the lead given by <a href="/wiki/Piedmont" title="Piedmont">Piedmont</a>, adopted the metric system in 1861.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metricItaly_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-metricItaly-53">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Exposition_Universelle_(1867)" title="Exposition Universelle (1867)">Exposition Universelle (1867)</a></i> (Paris Exhibition) devoted a stand to the metric system and by 1875, two thirds of the European population and close to half the world's population had adopted the metric system. By 1872, the only principal European countries not to have adopted the metric system were Russia and the United Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1920, countries comprising 22% of the world's population, mainly English-speaking, used the imperial system or the closely related US customary system; 25% used mainly the metric system and the remaining 53% used neither.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICB_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-NICB-46">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1927, several million people in the United States sent over 100,000 petitions backed by the Metric Association and The General Federation of Women's Clubs urging Congress to adopt the metric system. The petition was opposed by the manufacturing industry, citing the cost of the conversion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_standards">International standards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: International standards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1861 a committee of the <a href="/wiki/British_Science_Association" title="British Science Association">British Association for Advancement of Science (BAAS)</a> including William Thomson (later <a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">Lord Kelvin</a>), <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Prescott_Joule" title="James Prescott Joule">James Prescott Joule</a> introduced the concept of a coherent system of units based on the metre, gram and second, which, in 1873, was extended to include electrical units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[53]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Metric_seal.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="548" data-file-width="548" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Metric_seal.svg/220px-Metric_seal.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Metric_seal.svg/330px-Metric_seal.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Metric_seal.svg/440px-Metric_seal.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Metric_seal.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Seal of the <a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">International Bureau of Weights and Measures</a> (BIPM)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 20 May 1875 an international treaty known as the <i>Convention du Mètre</i> (<a href="/wiki/Metre_Convention" title="Metre Convention">Metre Convention</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[54]</a></sup> was signed by 17 states. This treaty established the following organisations to conduct international activities relating to a uniform system for measurements:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[55]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">General Conference on Weights and Measures</a> (CGPM),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-French_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-French-2">[Note 1]</a></sup> an intergovernmental conference of official delegates of member nations and the supreme authority for all actions;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Committee_for_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Committee for Weights and Measures">International Committee for Weights and Measures</a> (CIPM),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-French_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-French-2">[Note 1]</a></sup> consisting of selected scientists and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metrologist" title="Metrologist">metrologists</a>, which prepares and executes the decisions of the CGPM and is responsible for the supervision of the International Bureau of Weights and Measures (BIPM);</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">International Bureau of Weights and Measures</a> (BIPM),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-French_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-French-2">[Note 1]</a></sup> a permanent laboratory and world centre of scientific metrology, the activities of which include the establishment of the basic standards and scales of the principal physical quantities and maintenance of the international prototype standards.</li>
</ul>
<p>In 1881 first International Electrical Congress adopted the BAAS recommendations on electrical units, followed by a series of congresses in which further units of measure were defined and the <a href="/wiki/International_Electrotechnical_Commission" title="International Electrotechnical Commission">International Electrotechnical Commission</a> (IEC) was set up with the specific task of overseeing electrical units of measure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ieeeghn_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-ieeeghn-60">[56]</a></sup> This was followed by the <a href="/wiki/International_Congress_of_Radiology" title="International Congress of Radiology">International Congress of Radiology</a> (ISR) who, at their inaugural meeting in 1926, initiated the definition of radiological-related units of measure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISR_History_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISR_History-61">[57]</a></sup> In 1921 the Metre Convention was extended to cover all units of measure, not just length and mass and in 1933 the 8th CGPM resolved to work with other international bodies to agree standards for electrical units that could be related back to the international prototypes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[58]</a></sup> Since 1954 the CIPM committee that oversees the definition of units of measurement, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CIPM" title="CIPM">Consultative Committee for Units</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[Note 5]</a></sup> has representatives from many international organisations including the ISR, IEC and <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">ISO</a> under the chairmanship of the CIPM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Variants">Variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A number of variants of the metric system evolved, all using the <i>Mètre des Archives</i> and <i>Kilogramme des Archives</i> (or their descendants) as their base units, but differing in the definitions of the various derived units.</p>
