<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Centimetre-gram-second_systems">Centimetre-gram-second systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Centimetre-gram-second systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centimetre_gram_second_system_of_units" title="Centimetre gram second system of units">centimetre gram second system of units</a> (CGS) was the first coherent metric system, having been developed in the 1860s and promoted by Maxwell and Thomson. In 1874, this system was formally promoted by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Science" title="British Association for the Advancement of Science">British Association for the Advancement of Science</a> (BAAS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SI_1_8_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-SI_1_8-19">[17]</a></sup> The system's characteristics are that density is expressed in <span class="nowrap">g/cm<sup>3</sup></span>, force expressed in <a href="/wiki/Dyne" title="Dyne">dynes</a> and mechanical energy in <a href="/wiki/Erg" title="Erg">ergs</a>. Thermal energy was defined in <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">calories</a>, one calorie being the energy required to raise the temperature of one gram of water from 15.5 °C to 16.5 °C. The meeting also proposed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centimetre_gram_second_system_of_units" title="Centimetre gram second system of units">two sets of units for electrical and magnetic properties</a> – the electrostatic set of units and the electromagnetic set of units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metre-kilogram-second_systems">Metre-kilogram-second systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Metre-kilogram-second systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The CGS units of electricity were cumbersome to work with. This was remedied at the 1893 International Electrical Congress held in Chicago by defining the "international" ampere and ohm using definitions based on the <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">metre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilogram</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">second</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[61]</a></sup> In 1901, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Giorgi" title="Giovanni Giorgi">Giovanni Giorgi</a> showed that by adding an electrical unit as a fourth base unit, the various anomalies in electromagnetic systems could be resolved. The metre-kilogram-second-<a href="/wiki/Coulomb" title="Coulomb">coulomb</a> (MKSC) and metre-kilogram-second-<a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">ampere</a> (MKSA) systems are examples of such systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IECGiorgi_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-IECGiorgi-67">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">International System of Units</a> (<i>Système international d'unités</i> or SI) is the current international standard metric system and is also the system most widely used around the world. It is an extension of Giorgi's MKSA system—its base units are the metre, kilogram, second, ampere, <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Candela" title="Candela">candela</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mole</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SI_units_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-SI_units-22">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metre-tonne-second_systems">Metre-tonne-second systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Metre-tonne-second systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metre-tonne-second_system_of_units" title="Metre-tonne-second system of units">metre-tonne-second system of units</a> (MTS) was based on the metre, <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonne</a> and second – the unit of force was the <a href="/wiki/Sth%C3%A8ne" title="Sthène">sthène</a> and the unit of pressure was the <a href="/wiki/Pi%C3%A8ze" title="Pièze">pièze</a>. It was invented in France for industrial use and from 1933 to 1955 was used both in France and in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ieeeghn_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-ieeeghn-60">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational_systems">Gravitational systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Gravitational systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gravitational_metric_system" title="Gravitational metric system">Gravitational metric systems</a> use the <a href="/wiki/Kilogram-force" title="Kilogram-force">kilogram-force</a> (kilopond) as a base unit of force, with mass measured in a unit known as the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_metric_system" title="Gravitational metric system">hyl</a>, <i>Technische Mass Einheit</i> (TME), mug or <a href="/wiki/Slug_(unit)" title="Slug (unit)">metric slug</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[64]</a></sup> Although the CGPM passed a resolution in 1901 defining the standard value of <a href="/wiki/Standard_gravity" title="Standard gravity">acceleration due to gravity</a> to be 980.665 cm/s<sup>2</sup>, gravitational units are not part of the <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">International System of Units</a> (SI).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_System_of_Units">International System of Units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: International System of Units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">International System of Units</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_physical_quantities" title="List of physical quantities">List of physical quantities</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SI_Brochure_Cover.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="212" data-file-width="150" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/2b/SI_Brochure_Cover.jpg" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">Cover of brochure <i><a class="external text" href="http://www.bipm.org/en/publications/brochure/" rel="nofollow">The International System of Units</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 9th CGPM met in 1948, three years after the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> and fifteen years after the 8th CGPM. In response to formal requests made by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Physics" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Physics">International Union of Pure and Applied Physics</a> and by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_France" title="Cabinet of France">French Government</a> to establish a practical system of units of measure, the CGPM requested the CIPM to prepare recommendations for such a system, suitable for adoption by all countries adhering to the Metre Convention. The recommendation also catalogued symbols for the most important <a href="/wiki/MKS_system_of_units" title="MKS system of units">MKS</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centimetre_gram_second_system_of_units" title="Centimetre gram second system of units">CGS</a> units of measure and for the first time the CGPM made recommendations concerning derived units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[66]</a></sup> At the same time the CGPM formally adopted a recommendation for the writing and printing of unit symbols and of numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>The CIPM's draft proposal, which was an extensive revision and simplification of the metric unit definitions, symbols and terminology based on the MKS system of units, was put to the 10th CGPM in 1954. In accordance with Giorgi's proposals of 1901, the CIPM also recommended that the ampere be the base unit from which electromechanical units would be derived. The definitions for the ohm and volt that had previously been in use were discarded and these units became derived units based on the metre, ampere, second and kilogram. After negotiations with the <a href="/wiki/International_Commission_on_Illumination" title="International Commission on Illumination">International Commission on Illumination</a> (CIE)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-French_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-French-2">[Note 1]</a></sup> and IUPAP, two further base units, the degree kelvin and the candela were also proposed as base units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[68]</a></sup> The full system and name "Système International d'Unités" were adopted at the 11th CGPM in October 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[69]</a></sup> During the years that followed the definitions of the base units and particularly the methods of applying these definitions have been refined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>The formal definition of International System of Units (SI) along with the associated resolutions passed by the CGPM and the CIPM are published by the BIPM in brochure form at regular intervals. The eighth edition of the brochure <i>Le Système International d'Unités—The International System of Units</i> was published in 2006 and is available on the internet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[71]</a></sup> In October 2011, at the 24th <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CGPM" title="CGPM">CGPM</a> proposals were made to change the <a href="/wiki/Proposed_redefinition_of_SI_base_units" title="Proposed redefinition of SI base units">definitions</a> of four of the base units. These changes should not affect the average person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-draft_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-draft-77">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relating_SI_to_the_real_world">Relating SI to the real world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Relating SI to the real world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Non-SI_units_mentioned_in_the_SI" title="Non-SI units mentioned in the SI">Non-SI units mentioned in the SI</a></div>
<p>Although SI, as published by the CGPM, should, in theory, meet all the requirements of commerce, science and technology, certain units of measure have acquired such a position within the world community that it is likely they will be used for many years to come. In order that such units are used consistently around the world, the CGPM catalogued such units in Tables 6 to 9 of the SI brochure. These categories are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[73]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Non-SI units accepted for use with the International System of Units (Table 6)</b>. This list includes the hour and minute, the angular measures (degree, minute and second of arc) and the historic [non-coherent] metric units, the <a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">litre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">hectare</a> (originally agreed by the CGPM in 1879)</li>
<li><b>Non-SI units whose values in SI units must be obtained experimentally (Table 7)</b>. This list includes various units of measure used in atomic and nuclear physics and in astronomy such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dalton_(unit)" title="Dalton (unit)">dalton</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_mass" title="Electron mass">electron mass</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_volt" title="Electron volt">electron volt</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical unit</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar mass</a>, and a number of other units of measure that are well-established, but dependent on experimentally-determined physical quantities.</li>
<li><b>Other non-SI units (Table 8)</b>. This list catalogues a number of units of measure that have been used internationally in certain well-defined spheres including the <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bar</a> for pressure, the <a href="/wiki/%C3%85ngstr%C3%B6m" title="Ångström">ångström</a> for <a href="/wiki/Atomic_physics" title="Atomic physics">atomic physics</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">nautical mile</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Knot_(unit)" title="Knot (unit)">knot</a> in <a href="/wiki/Navigation" title="Navigation">navigation</a>.</li>
<li><b>Non-SI units associated with the CGS and the CGS-Gaussian system of units (Table 9)</b>. This table catalogues a number of units of measure based on the CGS system and dating from the nineteenth century. They appear frequently in the literature, but their continued use is discouraged by the CGPM.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Usage_around_the_world">Usage around the world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Usage around the world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Metrication" title="Metrication">Metrication</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text">
<div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>may overuse or misuse color, making it hard to understand for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Color-blind" title="Color-blind">color-blind</a> users</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please remove or fix instances of distracting or <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Colour_contrast" title="Wikipedia:Colour contrast">hard-to-read</a> colors. See <a href="/wiki/Category:Wikipedia_articles_with_colour_accessibility_problems" title="Category:Wikipedia articles with colour accessibility problems">the category page</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:COLOR" title="Wikipedia:COLOR">WP:COLOR</a> for guidelines.</span> <small><i>(February 2015)</i></small></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Metrication_by_year_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="940" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Metrication_by_year_map.svg/260px-Metrication_by_year_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Metrication_by_year_map.svg/390px-Metrication_by_year_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Metrication_by_year_map.svg/520px-Metrication_by_year_map.svg.png 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Metrication_by_year_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Countries by date of metrication. Colours red to green show the pattern of metrication from 1795 to 1998. Black identifies countries that have not adopted the metric system as the primary measurement system. White identifies countries that already used the metric system at the time they gained their independence</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The usage of the metric system varies around the world. According to the US Central Intelligence Agency's <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_Factbook" title="The World Factbook">Factbook</a></i> (2007), the International System of Units has been adopted as the official system of weights and measures by all nations in the world except for <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> (Burma), <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> and the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Factbook_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-World_Factbook-79">[74]</a></sup> while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NIST" title="NIST">NIST</a> has identified the United States as the only industrialised country where the metric system is not the predominant system of units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[75]</a></sup> However, reports published since 2007 hold this is no longer true of Myanmar or Liberia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[76]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Agence_France-Presse" title="Agence France-Presse">Agence France-Presse</a> report from 2010 stated that <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a> had passed a law to replace the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_system" title="Imperial system">imperial system</a> with the metric system thereby aligning its system of measurement with that used by its <a href="/wiki/Mano_River_Union" title="Mano River Union">Mano River Union</a> (MRU) neighbours <a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a> and Liberia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[Note 6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[77]</a></sup> Reports from Myanmar suggest that the country is also planning to adopt the metric system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the United States metric units, authorised by <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> in 1866,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H.R._596_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-H.R._596-3">[2]</a></sup> are widely used in science, medicine, military, and partially in industry, but <a href="/wiki/United_States_customary_units" title="United States customary units">customary units</a> predominate in household use. At retail stores the litre is a commonly used unit for volume, especially on bottles of beverages, and milligrams are used to denominate the amounts of medications, rather than grains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[80]</a></sup> On the other hand, non-metric units are used in certain regulated environments such as <a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">nautical miles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Knot_(unit)" title="Knot (unit)">knots</a> in international aviation. Resistance to metrication, particularly in the UK and the US, has been connected to the perceived cost involved, a sense of patriotism and lack of desire to conform internationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC2011_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2011-87">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the countries of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> the metric system has replaced the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_units" title="Imperial units">imperial system</a> by varying degrees: <a href="/wiki/Metrication_in_Australia" title="Metrication in Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metrication_in_New_Zealand" title="Metrication in New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and Commonwealth countries in Africa are almost totally metric, <a href="/wiki/Metrication_in_India" title="Metrication in India">India is mostly metric</a> while <a href="/wiki/Metrication_in_Canada" title="Metrication in Canada">Canada is partly metric</a>. In the United Kingdom, <a href="/wiki/Metrication_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Metrication in the United Kingdom">the metric system</a>, the use of which was first permitted for trade in 1864, is used in much government business, in most industries including building, health and engineering and for pricing by measure or weight in most trading situations, both wholesale and retail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[84]</a></sup> However the imperial system is widely used by the British public, such as feet and inches as a measurement of height, weight in stone and pounds, and is legally mandated in various cases, such as road-sign distances, which must be given in yards and miles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_1-13"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC2011_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2011-87">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMroads_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-WMroads-91">[85]</a></sup> In 2007, the European Commission announced that it was to abandon the requirement for metric-only labelling on packaged goods in the UK, and to allow dual metric–imperial marking to continue indefinitely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some other jurisdictions, such as Hong Kong, have laws mandating or permitting other systems of measurement in parallel with the metric system in some or all contexts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HK_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-HK-93">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p><span id="Symbol"></span><span id="Symbols"></span></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Variations_in_spelling">Variations in spelling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Variations in spelling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The SI symbols for the metric units are intended to be identical, regardless of the language used<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noun_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-noun-7">[6]</a></sup> but unit names are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_noun" title="Common noun">ordinary nouns</a> and use the character set and follow the grammatical rules of the language concerned. For example, the SI unit symbol for kilometre is "km" everywhere in the world, even though the local language word for the unit name may vary. Language variants for the kilometre unit name include: <i><span lang="it" xml:lang="it">chilometro</span></i> (Italian), <i><span lang="de" xml:lang="de">Kilometer</span></i> (German),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[Note 7]</a></sup> <i><span lang="nl" xml:lang="nl">kilometer</span></i> (Dutch), <i><span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">kilomètre</span></i> (French), <i><span lang="el" xml:lang="el">χιλιόμετρο</span></i> (Greek), <i><span lang="pt" xml:lang="pt">quilómetro/quilômetro</span></i> (Portuguese), <i><span lang="es" xml:lang="es">kilómetro</span></i> (Spanish) and <i><span lang="bg" xml:lang="bg">километр</span></i> (Russian).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Variations are also found with the spelling of unit names in countries using the same language, including differences in <a href="/wiki/American_and_British_English_spelling_differences#-re.2C_-er" title="American and British English spelling differences">American English and British spelling</a>. For example, <i>meter</i> and <i>liter</i> are used in the United States whereas <i>metre</i> and <i>litre</i> are used in other English-speaking countries. In addition, the official US spelling for the rarely used <a href="/wiki/Deca-" title="Deca-">SI prefix for ten</a> is <i>deka</i>. In American English the term <i>metric ton</i> is the normal usage whereas in other varieties of English <i>tonne</i> is common. <i>Gram</i> is also sometimes spelled <i>gramme</i> in English-speaking countries other than the United States, though this older usage is declining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conversion_and_calculation_incidents">Conversion and calculation incidents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Conversion and calculation incidents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The dual usage of or confusion between metric and non-metric units has resulted in a number of serious incidents. These include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Flying an overloaded American International Airways aircraft from <a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">Miami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maiquetia" title="Maiquetia">Maiquetia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> on 26 May 1994. The degree of overloading was consistent with ground crew reading the kilogram markings on the cargo as pounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[91]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1999 the <a href="/wiki/Patient_safety_organization#Institute_for_Safe_Medication_Practices" title="Patient safety organization">Institute for Safe Medication Practices</a> reported that confusion between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grain_(mass)" title="Grain (mass)">grains</a> and grams led to a patient receiving <a href="/wiki/Phenobarbital" title="Phenobarbital">phenobarbital</a> 0.5 grams instead of 0.5 grains (0.03 grams) after the practitioner misread the prescription.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[92]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Canadian "<a href="/wiki/Gimli_Glider" title="Gimli Glider">Gimli Glider</a>" accident in 1983, when a <a href="/wiki/Boeing_767" title="Boeing 767">Boeing 767</a> jet ran out of fuel in mid-flight because of two mistakes made when calculating the fuel supply of <a href="/wiki/Air_Canada" title="Air Canada">Air Canada</a>'s first aircraft to use metric measurements: mechanics miscalculated the amount of fuel required by the aircraft as a result of their unfamiliarity with metric units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li>The root cause of the loss in 1999 of <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s US$125 million <a href="/wiki/Mars_Climate_Orbiter" title="Mars Climate Orbiter">Mars Climate Orbiter</a> was a mismatch of units – the spacecraft engineers calculated the thrust forces required for velocity changes using US customary units (lbf·s) whereas the team who built the thrusters were expecting a value in metric units (N·s) as per the agreed specification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[95]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conversion_between_SI_and_legacy_units">Conversion between SI and legacy units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Conversion between SI and legacy units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conversion_of_units" title="Conversion of units">Conversion of units</a></div>
<p>During its evolution, the metric system has adopted many units of measure. The introduction of SI rationalised both the way in which units of measure were defined and also the list of units in use. These are now catalogued in the official SI Brochure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SI_units_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-SI_units-22">[20]</a></sup> The table below lists the units of measure in this catalogue and shows the conversion factors connecting them with the equivalent units that were in use on the eve of the adoption of SI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[99]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_developments">Future developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Future developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proposed_redefinition_of_SI_base_units" title="Proposed redefinition of SI base units">Proposed redefinition of SI base units</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Relations_between_New_SI_units_definitions.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="1920" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Relations_between_New_SI_units_definitions.svg/220px-Relations_between_New_SI_units_definitions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Relations_between_New_SI_units_definitions.svg/330px-Relations_between_New_SI_units_definitions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Relations_between_New_SI_units_definitions.svg/440px-Relations_between_New_SI_units_definitions.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Relations_between_New_SI_units_definitions.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Relations between proposed SI units definitions (in colour) and with seven fundamental constants of nature (in grey) with fixed numerical values in the proposed system.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the metre was redefined in 1960, the kilogram was the only SI base unit that relied on a specific artefact. After the 1996–1998 recalibrations a clear divergence between the international and various national prototype kilograms was observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the 23rd CGPM (2007), the CIPM was mandated to investigate the use of natural constants as the basis for all units of measure rather than the artefacts that were then in use. At a meeting of the CCU held in <a href="/wiki/Reading,_Berkshire" title="Reading, Berkshire">Reading, United Kingdom</a> in September 2010, a resolution<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[101]</a></sup> and draft changes to the SI brochure that were to be presented to the next meeting of the CIPM in October 2010 were agreed to in principle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-draft_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-draft-77">[72]</a></sup> The CCU proposed to</p>
<ul>
<li>in addition to the speed of light, define four constants of nature—<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_constant" title="Planck's constant">Planck's constant</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge">elementary charge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avogadro_constant" title="Avogadro constant">Avogadro constant</a> – to have exact values</li>
<li>retire the international prototype kilogram</li>
<li>revise the current definitions of the kilogram, <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">ampere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mole</a> to make use of the above four constants of nature</li>
<li>tighten the wording of the definitions of all the base units</li>
</ul>
<p>The CIPM meeting of October 2010 found that "the conditions set by the General Conference at its 23rd meeting have not yet been fully met. For this reason the CIPM does not propose a revision of the SI at the present time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[102]</a></sup> The CIPM did however sponsor a resolution at the 24th CGPM in which the changes were agreed in principle and that were expected to be finalised at the 25th CGPM in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[103]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-French-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-French_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-French_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-French_2-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-French_2-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-French_2-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-French_2-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The following abbreviations are taken from the French rather than the English language</span>

<li><span class="reference-text">SI: <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units"><i>Le Système international d'unités</i></a></span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text">CGPM: <a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures"><i>Conférence générale des poids et mesures</i></a></span></li>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Non-SI units for time and plane angle measurement, inherited from existing systems, are an exception to the decimal-multiplier rule</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-94">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In German all nouns start with an upper-case letter</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-107"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-107">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Roentgen_(unit)" title="Roentgen (unit)">Roentgen</a> is a measure of ionisation (charge per mass), not of absorbed dose, so there is no well-defined conversion factor. However, a radiation field of gamma rays that produces 1 roentgen of ionisation in dry air would deposit 0.0096 gray in soft tissue, and between 0.01 and 0.04 grays in bone. Since this unit was often used in radiation detectors, a factor of 0.01 can be used to convert the detector reading in roentgens to the approximate absorbed dose in grays.</span></li>
</ol>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metric_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
